Coats</a> of the U.S. Geological Survey described the main features of <a href="/wiki/Island_arc" title="Island arc">island arc</a> subduction in the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. His paper, though little noted (and even ridiculed) at the time, has since been called "seminal" and "prescient"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. In reality, it actually shows that the work by the European scientists on island arcs and mountain belts performed and published during the 1930s up until the 1950s was applied and appreciated also in the United States<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p><p>If Earth's crust was expanding along the oceanic ridges, Hess and Dietz reasoned like Holmes and others before them, it must be shrinking elsewhere. Hess followed Heezen, suggesting that new oceanic crust continuously spreads away from the ridges in a conveyor belt–like motion. And, using the mobilistic concepts developed before, he correctly concluded that many millions of years later, the oceanic crust eventually descends along the continental margins where oceanic trenches—very deep, narrow canyons—are formed, e.g. along <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Ring_of_Fire" title="Pacific Ring of Fire">the rim of the Pacific Ocean basin</a>. The important step Hess made was that convection currents would be the driving force in this process, arriving at the same conclusions as Holmes had decades before with the only difference that the thinning of the ocean crust was performed using Heezen's mechanism of spreading along the ridges. Hess therefore concluded that the Atlantic Ocean was expanding while the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> was shrinking. As old oceanic crust is "consumed" in the trenches (like Holmes and others, he thought this was done by thickening of the continental lithosphere, not, as now understood, by underthrusting at a larger scale of the oceanic crust itself into the mantle), new magma rises and erupts along the spreading ridges to form new crust. In effect, the ocean basins are perpetually being "recycled", with the forming of new crust and the destruction of old oceanic lithosphere occurring simultaneously. Thus, the new mobilistic concepts neatly explained why Earth does not get bigger with sea floor spreading, why there is so little sediment accumulation on the ocean floor, and why oceanic rocks are much younger than continental rocks.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_striping">Magnetic striping</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg/220px-Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg/330px-Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg/440px-Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic.Stripe.Magnetic.Anomalies.Scheme.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seafloor magnetic striping.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarityshift.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="144" data-file-width="192" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Polarityshift.gif" width="192"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarityshift.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A demonstration of magnetic striping. (The darker the color is, the closer it is to normal polarity)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vine%E2%80%93Matthews%E2%80%93Morley_hypothesis" title="Vine–Matthews–Morley hypothesis">Vine–Matthews–Morley hypothesis</a></div>
<p>Beginning in the 1950s, scientists like <a href="/wiki/Victor_Vacquier" title="Victor Vacquier">Victor Vacquier</a>, using magnetic instruments (<a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometers</a>) adapted from airborne devices developed during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> to detect <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>, began recognizing odd magnetic variations across the ocean floor. This finding, though unexpected, was not entirely surprising because it was known that <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>—the iron-rich, volcanic rock making up the ocean floor—contains a strongly magnetic mineral (<a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a>) and can locally distort compass readings. This distortion was recognized by Icelandic mariners as early as the late 18th century. More importantly, because the presence of magnetite gives the basalt measurable magnetic properties, these newly discovered magnetic variations provided another means to study the deep ocean floor. When newly formed rock cools, such magnetic materials recorded <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> at the time.
</p><p>As more and more of the seafloor was mapped during the 1950s, the magnetic variations turned out not to be random or isolated occurrences, but instead revealed recognizable patterns. When these magnetic patterns were mapped over a wide region, the ocean floor showed a <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebra</a>-like pattern: one stripe with normal polarity and the adjoining stripe with reversed polarity. The overall pattern, defined by these alternating bands of normally and reversely polarized rock, became known as magnetic striping, and was published by <a href="/wiki/Ron_G._Mason" title="Ron G. Mason">Ron G. Mason</a> and co-workers in 1961, who did not find, though, an explanation for these data in terms of sea floor spreading, like Vine, Matthews and Morley a few years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery of magnetic striping called for an explanation. In the early 1960s scientists such as Heezen, Hess and Dietz had begun to theorise that mid-ocean ridges mark structurally weak zones where the ocean floor was being ripped in two lengthwise along the ridge crest (see the previous paragraph). New <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> from deep within Earth rises easily through these weak zones and eventually erupts along the crest of the ridges to create new oceanic crust. This process, at first denominated the "conveyer belt hypothesis" and later called seafloor spreading, operating over many millions of years continues to form new ocean floor all across the 50,000 km-long system of mid-ocean ridges.
</p><p>Only four years after the maps with the "zebra pattern" of magnetic stripes were published, the link between sea floor spreading and these patterns was correctly placed, independently by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Morley" title="Lawrence Morley">Lawrence Morley</a>, and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Vine" title="Fred Vine">Fred Vine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drummond_Matthews" title="Drummond Matthews">Drummond Matthews</a>, in 1963,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVineMatthews1963_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVineMatthews1963-67">[67]</a></sup> now called the <a href="/wiki/Vine%E2%80%93Matthews%E2%80%93Morley_hypothesis" title="Vine–Matthews–Morley hypothesis">Vine–Matthews–Morley hypothesis</a>. This hypothesis linked these patterns to geomagnetic reversals and was supported by several lines of evidence:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>the stripes are symmetrical around the crests of the mid-ocean ridges; at or near the crest of the ridge, the rocks are very young, and they become progressively older away from the ridge crest;</li>
<li>the youngest rocks at the ridge crest always have present-day (normal) polarity;</li>
<li>stripes of rock parallel to the ridge crest alternate in magnetic polarity (normal-reversed-normal, etc.), suggesting that they were formed during different epochs documenting the (already known from independent studies) normal and reversal episodes of Earth's magnetic field.</li></ol>
<p>By explaining both the zebra-like magnetic striping and the construction of the mid-ocean ridge system, the seafloor spreading hypothesis (SFS) quickly gained converts and represented another major advance in the development of the plate-tectonics theory. Furthermore, the oceanic crust now came to be appreciated as a natural "tape recording" of the history of the geomagnetic field reversals (GMFR) of Earth's magnetic field. Today, extensive studies are dedicated to the calibration of the normal-reversal patterns in the oceanic crust on one hand and known timescales derived from the dating of basalt layers in sedimentary sequences (<a href="/wiki/Magnetostratigraphy" title="Magnetostratigraphy">magnetostratigraphy</a>) on the other, to arrive at estimates of past spreading rates and plate reconstructions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_refining_of_the_theory">Definition and refining of the theory</span></h3>
<p>After all these considerations, Plate Tectonics (or, as it was initially called "New Global Tectonics") became quickly accepted in the scientific world, and numerous papers followed that defined the concepts:
</p>
<ul><li>In 1965, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuzo_Wilson" title="Tuzo Wilson">Tuzo Wilson</a> who had been a promoter of the sea floor spreading hypothesis and continental drift from the very beginning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1963_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1963-69">[69]</a></sup> added the concept of <a href="/wiki/Transform_fault" title="Transform fault">transform faults</a> to the model, completing the classes of fault types necessary to make the mobility of the plates on the globe work out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1965_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1965-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
<li>A symposium on continental drift was held at the Royal Society of London in 1965 which must be regarded as the official start of the acceptance of plate tectonics by the scientific community, and which abstracts are issued as <a href="#CITEREFBlackettBullardRuncorn1965">Blackett, Bullard &amp; Runcorn (1965)</a>. In this symposium, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bullard" title="Edward Bullard">Edward Bullard</a> and co-workers showed with a computer calculation how the continents along both sides of the Atlantic would best fit to close the ocean, which became known as the famous "Bullard's Fit".</li>
<li>In 1966 Wilson published the paper that referred to previous plate tectonic reconstructions, introducing the concept of what is now known as the "<a href="/wiki/Wilson_Cycle" title="Wilson Cycle">Wilson Cycle</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1966_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1966-71">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1967, at the <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a>'s meeting, <a href="/wiki/W._Jason_Morgan" title="W. Jason Morgan">W. Jason Morgan</a> proposed that Earth's surface consists of 12 rigid plates that move relative to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1968_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1968-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two months later, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Le_Pichon" title="Xavier Le Pichon">Xavier Le Pichon</a> published a complete model based on six major plates with their relative motions, which marked the final acceptance by the scientific community of plate tectonics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELe_Pichon1968_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELe_Pichon1968-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Dan_McKenzie_(geophysicist)" title="Dan McKenzie (geophysicist)">McKenzie</a> and Parker independently presented a model similar to Morgan's using translations and rotations on a sphere to define the plate motions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKenzieParker1967_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKenzieParker1967-74">[74]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_Tectonics_Revolution">Plate Tectonics Revolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plate_Tectonics_Revolution" title="Plate Tectonics Revolution">Plate Tectonics Revolution</a></div>
<p>The Plate Tectonics Revolution was the scientific and cultural change which developed from the acceptance of the plate tectonics theory. The event was a <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm shift</a> and scientific revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implications_for_biogeography">Implications for biogeography</span></h2>
<p>Continental drift theory helps biogeographers to explain the disjunct <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeographic</a> distribution of present-day life found on different continents but having <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">similar ancestors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMossWilson1998_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMossWilson1998-76">[76]</a></sup> In particular, it explains the Gondwanan distribution of <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">Antarctic flora</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_reconstruction">Plate reconstruction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plate_reconstruction" title="Plate reconstruction">Plate reconstruction</a></div>
<p>Reconstruction is used to establish past (and future) plate configurations, helping determine the shape and make-up of ancient supercontinents and providing a basis for paleogeography.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_plate_boundaries">Defining plate boundaries</span></h3>
<p>Current plate boundaries are defined by their seismicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECondie1997_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECondie1997-77">[77]</a></sup> Past plate boundaries within existing plates are identified from a variety of evidence, such as the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophiolites" title="Ophiolites">ophiolites</a> that are indicative of vanished oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELliboutry2000_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELliboutry2000-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Past_plate_motions">Past plate motions</span></h3>
<p>Tectonic motion is believed to have begun around 3 to 3.5 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kranendonk2011_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kranendonk2011-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The reason for this is unclear. (December 2014)">why?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Various types of quantitative and semi-quantitative information are available to constrain past plate motions. The geometric fit between continents, such as between west Africa and South America is still an important part of plate reconstruction. Magnetic stripe patterns provide a reliable guide to relative plate motions going back into the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The tracks of hotspots give absolute reconstructions, but these are only available back to the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorsvikSteinberger2008_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorsvikSteinberger2008-82">[82]</a></sup> Older reconstructions rely mainly on paleomagnetic pole data, although these only constrain the latitude and rotation, but not the longitude. Combining poles of different ages in a particular plate to produce apparent polar wander paths provides a method for comparing the motions of different plates through time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1992_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1992-83">[83]</a></sup> Additional evidence comes from the distribution of certain <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> types,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> faunal provinces shown by particular fossil groups, and the position of <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogenic belts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorsvikSteinberger2008_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorsvikSteinberger2008-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_break-up_of_continents">Formation and break-up of continents</span></h3>
<p>The movement of plates has caused the formation and break-up of continents over time, including occasional formation of a <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> that contains most or all of the continents. The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a> or Nuna formed during a period of <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2000–1800">2,000 to 1,800</a> million years ago</span> and broke up about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1500–1300">1,500 to 1,300</a> million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhaoCawoodWildeSun2002_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhaoCawoodWildeSun2002-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhaoSunWildeLi2004_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhaoSunWildeLi2004-86">[86]</a></sup> The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a> is thought to have formed about 1 billion years ago and to have embodied most or all of Earth's continents, and broken up into eight continents around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=600">600</a> million years ago</span>. The eight continents later re-assembled into another supercontinent called <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>; Pangaea broke up into <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasia</a> (which became North America and Eurasia) and <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> (which became the remaining continents).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, the world's tallest mountain range, are assumed to have been formed by the collision of two major plates. Before uplift, they were covered by the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_plates">Current plates</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_tectonic_plates" title="List of tectonic plates">List of tectonic plates</a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plate_tectonics_map.gif" title="Plate tectonics map"><img alt="Plate tectonics map" data-file-height="2285" data-file-width="3380" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/440px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/660px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/880px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif 2x" width="440"/></a></div>
<p>Depending on how they are defined, there are usually seven or eight "major" plates: <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian</a>. The latter is sometimes subdivided into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian</a> plates.
</p><p>There are dozens of smaller plates, the seven largest of which are the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Plate" title="Arabian Plate">Arabian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Plate" title="Caribbean Plate">Caribbean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Fuca_Plate" title="Juan de Fuca Plate">Juan de Fuca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Plate" title="Cocos Plate">Cocos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">Nazca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Plate" title="Scotia Plate">Scotia</a>.
</p><p>The current motion of the tectonic plates is today determined by remote sensing satellite data sets, calibrated with ground station measurements.
</p>
<h2><span id="Other_celestial_bodies_.28planets.2C_moons.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_celestial_bodies_(planets,_moons)">Other celestial bodies (planets, moons)</span></h2>
<p>The appearance of plate tectonics on <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> is related to planetary mass, with <a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">more massive planets than Earth</a> expected to exhibit plate tectonics. Earth may be a borderline case, owing its tectonic activity to abundant water (silica and water form a deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic" title="Eutectic">eutectic</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValenciaO'ConnellSasselov2007_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValenciaO'ConnellSasselov2007-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">Venus</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Venus" title="Geology of Venus">Geology of Venus</a></div>
<p>Venus shows no evidence of active plate tectonics. There is debatable evidence of active tectonics in the planet's distant past; however, events taking place since then (such as the plausible and generally accepted hypothesis that the Venusian <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> has thickened greatly over the course of several hundred million years) has made constraining the course of its geologic record difficult. However, the numerous well-preserved <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> have been utilized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dating_methods" title="Dating methods">dating method</a> to approximately date the Venusian surface (since there are thus far no known samples of Venusian rock to be dated by more reliable methods). Dates derived are dominantly in the range <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=500–750">500 to 750</a> million years ago</span>, although ages of up to <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,200">1,200</a> million years ago</span> have been calculated. This research has led to the fairly well accepted hypothesis that Venus has undergone an essentially complete volcanic resurfacing at least once in its distant past, with the last event taking place approximately within the range of estimated surface ages. While the mechanism of such an impressive thermal event remains a debated issue in Venusian geosciences, some scientists are advocates of processes involving plate motion to some extent.
</p><p>One explanation for Venus's lack of plate tectonics is that on Venus temperatures are too high for significant water to be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKasting1988_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKasting1988-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Earth's crust is soaked with water, and water plays an important role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Shear_zone" title="Shear zone">shear zones</a>. Plate tectonics requires weak surfaces in the crust along which crustal slices can move, and it may well be that such weakening never took place on Venus because of the absence of water. However, some researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> remain convinced that plate tectonics is or was once active on this planet.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">Mars</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">Geology of Mars</a></div>
<p>Mars is considerably smaller than Earth and Venus, and there is evidence for ice on its surface and in its crust.
</p><p>In the 1990s, it was proposed that <a href="/wiki/Martian_dichotomy" title="Martian dichotomy">Martian Crustal Dichotomy</a> was created by plate tectonic processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESleep1994_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESleep1994-90">[90]</a></sup> Scientists today disagree, and think that it was created either by upwelling within the Martian <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> that thickened the crust of the Southern Highlands and formed <a href="/wiki/Tharsis" title="Tharsis">Tharsis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhongZuber2001_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhongZuber2001-91">[91]</a></sup> or by a giant impact that excavated the <a href="/wiki/Vastitas_Borealis" title="Vastitas Borealis">Northern Lowlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrews-HannaZuberBanerdt2008_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrews-HannaZuberBanerdt2008-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a> may be a tectonic boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tectonic_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-tectonic-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Observations made of the magnetic field of Mars by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a></i> spacecraft in 1999 showed patterns of magnetic striping discovered on this planet. Some scientists interpreted these as requiring plate tectonic processes, such as seafloor spreading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> However, their data fail a "magnetic reversal test", which is used to see if they were formed by flipping polarities of a global magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2000_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2000-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Icy_satellites">Icy satellites</span></h3>
<p>Some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_satellites" title="Galilean satellites">satellites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> have features that may be related to plate-tectonic style deformation, although the materials and specific mechanisms may be different from plate-tectonic activity on Earth. On 8 September 2014, NASA reported finding evidence of plate tectonics on <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, a satellite of Jupiter—the first sign of subduction activity on another world other than Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140908_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140908-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, the largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, was reported to show tectonic activity in images taken by the <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a>, which landed on Titan on January 14, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoderblomTomaskoArchinalBecker2007_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoderblomTomaskoArchinalBecker2007-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoplanets">Exoplanets</span></h3>
<p>On Earth-sized planets, plate tectonics is more likely if there are oceans of water. However, in 2007, two independent teams of researchers came to opposing conclusions about the likelihood of plate tectonics on larger <a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earths</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> with one team saying that plate tectonics would be episodic or stagnant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and the other team saying that plate tectonics is very likely on super-earths even if the planet is dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValenciaO'ConnellSasselov2007_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValenciaO'ConnellSasselov2007-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Consideration of plate tectonics is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">search for extraterrestrial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>

(2001). <a class="external text" href="http://www-geodyn.mit.edu/mars.deg1.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Degree-1 mantle convection and the crustal dichotomy on Mars"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_and_Planetary_Science_Letters" title="Earth and Planetary Science Letters">Earth and Planetary Science Letters</a></i>. <b>189</b> (1–2): 75–84. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2001E&amp;PSL.189...75Z" rel="nofollow">2001E&amp;PSL.189...75Z</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CiteSeerX_(identifier)" title="CiteSeerX (identifier)">CiteSeerX</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//citeseerx.ist.psu.edu/viewdoc/summary?doi=10.1.1.535.8224" rel="nofollow">10.1.1.535.8224</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0012-821X%2801%2900345-4" rel="nofollow">10.1016/S0012-821X(01)00345-4</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Earth+and+Planetary+Science+Letters&amp;rft.atitle=Degree-1+mantle+convection+and+the+crustal+dichotomy+on+Mars&amp;rft.volume=189&amp;rft.issue=1%E2%80%932&amp;rft.pages=75-84&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fciteseerx.ist.psu.edu%2Fviewdoc%2Fsummary%3Fdoi%3D10.1.1.535.8224%23id-name%3DCiteSeerX&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2FS0012-821X%2801%2900345-4&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2001E%26PSL.189...75Z&amp;rft.aulast=Zhong&amp;rft.aufirst=Shijie&amp;rft.au=Zuber%2C+Maria+T.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww-geodyn.mit.edu%2Fmars.deg1.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APlate+tectonics"></span></li></ul>
